0307477479|2.0|The one thing, it seems to me, about penning a multiple character novel is to not only make them interesting but to weave them into the plot to where it makes sense and ultimately serves a function in the outcome. This work by Jennifer Egan fails miserably on all accounts. It does have multiple characters, but by page 100, I'd challenge that the reader not only cannot keep track of them anymore, he doesn't care about them either. Burden on the reader never seemed to be a concern here and as a result, this book suffers mightily for it.<br /><br />The story seems loosely arranged around an aging record producer who once played bass guitar in a punk band and his now disaffected secretary/assistant. Neither of these warrant a connection with the reader and then we're soon inundated with peripheral characters that have even less charm. The writing is, at best, pedestrian while chapter after chapter go off on meaningless tangents...we see one former band member, now obese, planning a \\"Suicide Tour\\", but this just tails off. Another arcane segment deals with one character's older married man lover suddenly in Africa on some irrelevant trip with his children. Never do these or a host of other lines of story come to any conclusion...again I believe the author attempts to carry the narrative with flowing writing and character depth. A colossal failure.<br /><br />The most mind boggling feature about this work is that it won the Pulitzer Prize! I searched and searched for depth and meaning here but found just drivel. I will give kudos to Egan for attempting something groundbreaking, though, with a chapter laid out as a Power Point presentation but even that gets quickly overwhelming. Reminding me of the pages long rant at the end of another highly overrated novel, Atlas Shrugged (by Ayn Rand) in terms of tediousness, this is not only quickly ineffective, but exceedingly laborious to read and comprehend. Again no consideration to reader burden.<br /><br />Certainly tastes makebooks and evidently many enjoyed this work but this reviewer was very disappointed and shocked at the notion that this could even be nominated for the lofty Pulitzer, let alone win it. If I had to do it over again, I would definitely pass.

0062014102|2.0|I purchased this book recently after a rekindling of interest in reality theories.  Sometime last year I became enthralled with reality and consciousness after a friend introduced me to the oddities of physical material at the quantum level.  I've been reading books since then that seek to find a connect between the knowable scientific and material world and the spiritual or abstract dimensions of consciousness or the \\"universe\\" so to speak.<br /><br />What I was looking for, and what I'm afraid many people may also be looking for when reading the subtitle \\"A revolutionary theory of reality that explains the latest frontiers of physics,...\\" etc. was an actual plausible theory and concrete explanation of Talbot's holographic theory, backed by some degree of scientific inquiry, or barring that, a theory backed by logical observation.<br /><br />What I got was a book that is 75 pages of moderately interesting descriptions of Talbot's vision of the Holographic \\"theory\\" with a solid few pages of concrete science followed by 225 pages of the most absolutely useless anecdotal and incidental support for it that could possibly have ever been amassed.  Faith healers, disappearing acts, acid trips, stigmata, and even Talbot's own self proclaimed ability to move objects with his enormous brain are all touted as \\"factual evidence\\" of the existence of the holographic model chapter after increasingly irrelevant chapter.  There is scarcely a drop of hard scientific information provided in the entire book, and Talbot rarely makes time to analyze one of his witch-doctor voodoo references for validity before immediately confirming it as concrete proof of his holographic universe.<br /><br />The book proposed an interesting idea that I am sincerely interested in furthering, failed to fully develop the idea into anything plausible, and never even approached the integration of science into the \\"theory\\".  If you're into tarot cards and palm reading and religions experiences brought on byhallucinogenic drugs then this really will be an entertaining book.  But if you're curious about the plausibility of different reality theories scientifically, or looking for any sort of large scale validity whatsoever in an alternate theory of consciousness and reality, save your money.  You won't find it in this book.

0307743659|5.0|The Shining is a 1977 horror novel by Stephen King. It is about a man named Jack who goes to an isolated hotel with his wife and son so he can write a novel and not get writer's block. His son Danny has a strange power and he speaks to a spirit of some sort. When they get to the hotel, Jack slowly starts going crazy as the hotel is haunted with evil spirits that are manipulating him into doing harmful things. I love both the novel and the Stanley Kubrick film, but I want to mention the differences between the two.<br /><br />Differences between the novel and the 1980 Stanley Kubrick film:<br /><br />1. The novel shows many scenes where the characters are talking in their heads.<br /><br />2. The haunted room is called room 217 in the novel, and room 237 in the film.<br /><br />3. In the novel, both of Danny's parents know about his telepathic powers.<br /><br />4. The novel is much more violent than the film. The baseball bat scene is much more gruesome. In the novel, Jack breaks Wendy's ribs with the bat, then she smashes an object on his head, knocking him out.<br /><br />5. In the novel, we get a scene where Danny sees Tony in person and is speaking to him.<br /><br />6. The film doesn't adapt any of the parts with the guy in the bondage dog costume. Including the creepy scene where the guy is covered in blood tells Danny he's gonna eat him. We do see him in one scene though where he is doing something suggestive with his master.<br /><br />7. In the film, Hallorann is killed with an axe, but in the novel, Hallorann doesn't die.<br /><br />8. The ending of the film is completely different from the ending of the novel. At the end of the novel, the hotel explodes while Jack is inside. Wendy, Danny, and Hallorann escape alive.<br /><br />So I just wanted to mention those differences. Both the novel and film are terrific.<br /><br />All in all, Stephen King's The Shining is a really good and classic horror novel that horror fans should read.

B00BR0SGN0|4.0|I do not have the enhanced edition, so I am reviewing the basic CD release, which has one less track and no DVD. The 20 minute interview and live performances of older Purple songs didn't seem to me to be worth the extra 6 or 7 dollars.<br /><br />All in all, a nice CD. It takes some repeated listens to grow on you. It's not your mother's Deep Purple, but it is very good Deep Purple nonetheless. The sound/mix is superb, and there seems to be more of a leash on Steve Morse's guitar playing, i.e. more emphasis on the melodic as opposed to the the speed exercises. Don Airey does fine as we hear some good Hammond organ, and nice keys, and he gets some time to really stretch out (\\"A Simple Song\\")and shine. He is not Jon Lord, but no one else would be. He has his own sound.<br /><br />Gillan's vocals are at times thin and reedy, but the guy is in his late 60s so give him a break. Producer Bob Ezrin makes ample use of double tracking and vocal harmonies to bolster the singing, and it works. Gillan puts out a few dumb lyrics, but as Gillan said, the lyrics sometimes are there for percussive sound rather than meaning. The times that Gillan goes to his lower range, expecially on \\"All The Time In The World\\" and in the album opener \\"Simple Song,\\" his voice sounds full with nice grain and texture.<br /><br />I have read people saying it doesn't sound like \\"old\\" Deep Purple and still cry for the return of Ritchie Blackmore. Well, Ritchie hasn't been in the band for 19 years. Get over it. He's not coming back. Ever. Blackmore currently has an intense hatred of Gillan and will \\"never forgive\\" Roger Glover for the remixes he did on MACHINE HEAD. That bridge is long burned, and at age 68, Blackmore seems happy with what he is doing. Steve does fine, and his playing here is some of his best, as it seems to be, as stated earlier, a little more restrained. With Airey and Morse on board, it isn't going to sound like \\"old\\" Deep Purple.<br /><br />The songs are the best since the PERPENDICULAR album. I especially like the haunting parts of \\"A Simple Song.\\" The production shines and is clear. If anything, BANANAS and RAPTURE OF THE DEEP were muddy affairs, especially the drum sound. It is one of the best sounding records they have made. Producer Ezrin must have had some pull and influence, as he is credited as a co-writer along with the other band members.<br /><br />If you are a fan of the \\"Steve Morse\\" era of Deep Purple, you are going to like this. For the die hard Blackmore fans, probably nothing that the band does now will please you. I guess it's a matter of taste.<br /><br />Of course, this is a review of a rabid fan, who has loved all stages of this band.

0399165274|5.0|Forsyth came to fame in 1971 with The Day of the Jackal, a gripping thriller about the search for an anonymous contract killer who had been paid to assassinate French president Charles De Gaulle. He deserved the fame he got for the book because it really was good! That was his second book and first novel. Since then, a string of others has followed of exceptionally high quality.  So here we are , seventeen books and fourteen novels later,and here  is The Kill List, which is another knockout spy thriller by Forsyth.<br /><br />You don't read Forsyth novels for lush description or depth of the character development. Basically, you read them for plot. Forsyth is a pro at laying out exciting but credible high action narratives, based on the most recent of events and thus exceedingly relevant. He's an info wonk who knows and can explicate, without excess verbiage, the details that lend credence to his sometimes exceedingly complicated stories: in this current one, the technology of modern long distance spying, the ins and outs of politics (national and international), what to do if you forced to negotiate with Somali pirates, how to carry off a successful high risk \\"black op\\" while keeping it well below the radar screen.<br /><br />Forsyth is a bare bones, no nonsense kind of writer who tells you only as much about his protagonists as you need in order to understand what they're doing and why they did it. He's not all that interested in local color but a fair amount of it still appears in his novels any way because his plots move across colorful and often dangerous terrains.<br /><br />Kill List is about the hunt for an Arab terrorist, named the \\"Preacher,\\" who has been inciting young Muslims in the United States and Britain to commit random individual acts of terrorism. (There have been seven killings in the States and four in Great Britain.) The Preacher has learned from his predecessors, who made the mistake of letting themselves become visible and thus reachable.  As a consequence, the Preacher operates solely over the Internet with his face  masked. He has no apparent contact with disciples. He doesn't even use a cell phone because its  signal can be isolated by high flying satellite spy planes and subsequent calls traced.<br /><br />After a series of assassinations in the States, provoked by the Preacher's rantings against the West, the Preacher's name is placed on a super secret, eminently deniable Kill List, which is composed solely of enemies deemed so dangerous to our country that their deaths have been authorized ahead of time, with no questions asked. The agency responsible for dealing them bears the innocuous name of Technical Operations Support Activity (TOSA) but its operations are decidedly  lethal. 193700788X|1.0|I've been avoiding this because I thought some distance might make me feel better about this final effort.  Sadly, even Oklahoma wouldn't be enough distance for me to feel differently about Dead Ever After.<br /><br />First, let me preface this review with the fact that I loved this series up to and including Dead Reckoning (gave it four stars, despite the headscratching hidey-hole interaction with Bill).  I've loved the ethical issues the author presented and they way Sookie faced them, while not always head-on, with her own code of honor. I loved that the author said she intended the series to have a theme of tolerance and diversity.  I loved that an outcast in her human community could find a place where she was appreciated for who she was, despite not being the same as everyone else.  I had hoped at the end that Sookie would embrace her \\"essential spark\\" but it was not to be.<br /><br />About my disappointments.  *sigh*<br /><br />SPOILERS<br /><br />Honestly, perhaps my expectations were much too high but I know Charlaine Harris can write a hugely entertaining and satisfying book.  She's had years to think about the resolution of the series so I was expecting, and touting to others, a \\"knock it out of the ballpark\\" book.  My biggest disappointment is the feeling I had that she couldn't be bothered to write an Altogether Dead or even a Living Dead in Dallas.  She has obviously moved on.<br /><br />It also feels like she has taken the opportunity in DEA to take intentional jabs at some of her most ardent (well, those who were among her most ardent) fans.  \\"Literary fangbanger\\", what was the point of *that*?<br /><br />Sookie begins the series as someone who has been victimized and marginalized all of her life.  She ends the series back where she began.  She gives up what we heard from her in DR that she really wanted with no fight whatsoever, has sex with her best friend (not sexy sex, just sex), is going to be living right across the cemetary from the vampire who raped her and now has a legitimate purpose to continue lurking in her woods.  At the end, Sookie's just grateful to fit in with the miscreants who shunned her until she became like them herself, or at the very least appeared to be like them.  Sookie's as grateful for what she has in BT as she was for that regifted crockpot her bff (whose life she saved and who *owns* a women's boutique) gave her for her birthday.  She has no more self-esteem at the end than she did at the beginning.  I never thought I'd see Sookie as a victim despite all of her unfortunate experiences.<br /><br />Eric's gets virtually the same regifted crockpot.  He is under the thumb of a vampire queen, loses his \\"heart's desire\\", gives up what he values most (owning his life as much as he can) to protect the people he is responsible for.  He's Freyda's boy toy for a hundred years and gives up an extra hundred to try to arrange protection for Sookie and loses his children in the process.  We can feel good about though, right?  Um, no, it's not like FdC has been shown to make good on his debts...<br /><br />Sam, poor Sam.  He also gets the regifted crockpot.  Sookie's not passionate about him.  She's not even \\"in love\\" with him.  Eric's gone and he's just the next guy in line.<br /><br />Bill is a rapist.  He raped Sookie and now he's lurking in her woods until she dies or he gets staked.  I guess though he also gets the regifted crockpot.  He's not ever going to taste the sweetness of Sookie's fairy blood again . . . unless a nest of his buddies gets burned out, or he gets tortured and starved and can't help himself.  Just saying it's not beyond the realm of my imagination.  I'm sure we'll find in After Dead that he's BT's most sought after babysitter;)<br /><br />Finally, I guess my biggest disappointment is the message about diversity and tolerance this series leaves. You know, the one the author stated she intended?  I guess I thought it wasn't going to be that the vampires are too dangerous to live in human society, mainstreaming was an illusion, love for someone who is different from you is just too hard.  Sookie ends up with someone (maybe) that is most like her.  Supernatural but who looks human . . . most of the time.  In the end, does she even realize the vampires' have souls?  There seems to be some kind of religious message in this book as well, but I can't decipher what it is.  Apparently, someone needs to work on writing subtext.<br /><br />Not even going to mention continuity errors because it happens in this series and it's never been a fatal fail for me before.  Well, maybe I will mention one;)  Sookie tells Sam she can't read his mind in DEA but we know she can.  She read his mind about his trouble paying the beer distributor's bill.  She can read Bernie's (Sam's full shifter mom)in Small Town Wedding (although she prevaricates here too and tells Bernie it's hard to read shifters -- just after she's done so).  She reads Sam's detailed thoughts clearly several times in If I Had A Hammer, which Harris says is set after the end of the series.  So, either there is a huge continuity lapse here or Sookie lies to Sam like she did to his mother, to make them feel more comfortable around her I suppose.<br /><br />I've spent a huge portion of my sizeable book budget on this author's works over the past six years.  The bottom line (literal and metaphorical) is that's not ever going to happen again. 0062197266|5.0|More of a 4.5 stars, because this one was a bit tricky. Into the Darkest Corner is a engrossing cautionary tale of romance and obsession. It deals with loads of psychological issues in both their short and long term effects, and it was just so completely unputdownable I spent several hours in which I should have been studying for finals with my eyes stuck to Cathy Bailey's story.<br /><br />Basically, picture the following: You meet a guy, an intensely attractive and seemingly perfect guy, and ,out of some improbable conjunction of stars or rare alignment of planets, you both start dating. Your friends love him, he loves you, and it's all just red-paper hearts and champagne bottles, up until Perfect Guy starts veering a bit to the odd side. He doesn't like it when you go out with your friends without him, he has some very strong suggestions as to what you should wear, and, occasionally, the rough sex he has with you is less Fifty Shades of Gray than it is Fifthy Shades of Sore. Things get out of control and soon you find yourself beat up and traumatized, among other things, and as it manages to do once in a while, the system works and your ex goes to prison. Three years later, you're a different person, scared of people who aren't there, checking locks you know you already locked. Three years later, you might finally find happiness again. But three years later, is also when he gets out of jail, and you just know he's coming back for you.<br /><br />Into The Darkest Corner was fraught with tension and good writing, and despite veering towards the not-so-great as it neared its end, the book still had me hooked all through its 497 pages, plus it ended in a moment of ominous foreboding. I worried with Cathy, laughed with her, felt sorry for her, and was rejoiced to see her life finally make a turn for the better. As is the case with most thrillers, the ending doesn't make justice to the rest of the book, but despite that I burned through Into The Darkest Corner faster than I would most good books, and it is one of the best debuts I've read so far, along with Gillian Flynn's Sharp Objects and S.J. Watson's Before I Go To Sleep.<br /><br />I don't believe this is a book for everyone, due to the heavy domestic violence it portrays, but I don't think it ought to be a issue for most, especially since Ms. Haynes manages to weave lighthearted, romantic moments perfectly with instances of either spine-tingling suspense or shocking violence. If you are looking for a thriller that will grab you by the neck and won't let go of it until all the pages are past you and you're left panting for air, Into the Darkest Corner is it.

The highs are too brittle, the lows are a touch too high and the bass guitar isn't even out on the forefront. It's like they took the original master and just did a U-curve on a 10-band EQ with it, with the low/high being brought up and the middle section sloping down. Where's the warmth?<br /><br />I give this three stars because I really liked the packaging. I thought the poster and cards were cool and most importantly - THE LIVE DISC! What's the point of a special record store day release if you're just going to put it on iTunes a month later? That's not very special.<br /><br />Will always love R.E.M., have all of thier albums. I have given them a lot of my money over the years. B00BYISQTO|5.0|(Reviewed by Connie's son)<br /><br />*This review is largely subjective. Opinions may clash like socks and flip-flops.*<br /><br />\\"For the 12 years we've been making records, we've always been trying to prove something, and avoid being labeled as dad rock, or depressing,\\" Berninger says. \\"We figured out how to fight so much over the years... This time around, we didn't care.\\"<br /><br />Matt Berninger revealed in an article posted by Billboard on May 20, 2013 that the band had been trying to fight a certain label for years. On The National's new album, \\"Trouble Will Find Me,\\" the band has been alleviated of the pressure that was especially prominent during the making of their previous album, \\"High Violet.\\" In addition to having a better time making the music found on their newest album, they've also done much more experimenting. In the end, this album sounds just as good as The National has always sounded, but with a few new tricks up its collective sleeve.<br /><br />...And here comes the ridiculously long track-by-track segment.<br /><br />----SONG 1: \\"I Should Live in Salt\\"<br /><br />Written in [C Major], the song opens the album pleasantly. The song doesn't seem like much to swoon over immediately, but after multiple listens, the track unfurls and shows its structural trickery, instrumental flourishes, and lyrical quality.<br /><br />ANALYSIS: One thing stands out in the track immediately: a tricky extra beat added to the song.  The repeating progression feels like 4/4, 5/4, 4/4, 4/4. However, it isn't to extremely jarring effect, and it's certainly an interesting touch. After enough listens, it becomes very natural feeling and the listener will most likely forget that the extra beat is there (Arcade Fire's \\"Modern Man,\\" anyone?). Sufjan Stevens' synth flourishes add nicely to the song, especially at the end, where those flourishes are key. There's also a moment where a touch of accordian is used to good effect during one of the verses (not profound, but oddly memorable anyway). Perhaps the most memorable part of the song, however, is the booming chorus, which unearths the previously hidden gems of Matt Berninger's vocal range (the result of quitting smoking) and echoes in the listener's head after repeated listens. It lords over the song, like a reigning, and maybe regretful, king.<br /><br />VERDICT: It may not seem like much at a first glance, but the song is hiding a lot of qualities that deserve to be noticed. Those qualities make this song a worthy opener. Watch out for the grandiose, bathos-laden chorus.<br /><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"Learn to appreciate the void / You should know me better than that\\"<br />2.) \\"I should leave it alone but you're not right\\"<br />3.) \\"We have different enemies / You should know me better than that\\"<br /><br />----SONG 2: \\"Demons\\"<br /><br />Written in [G Major], the second song is somber (and somewhat sinister sounding) but surprisingly rattling on the first listen and an extremely bold move as the first single. The time signature completely kicks 4/4 in the face, yet once the listener becomes accustomed to it, the numbness of difference subsides and the song reveals its beauties and solidifies its position as one of the most important songs on the album. Also, the lyrics.<br /><br />ANALYSIS:  The listener's brain may be counting in 4/4 on the first listen, but before they know it, they're lost. The song blindsides the listener with 7/4 throughout, challenging their brain to follow along. After several rounds, the song grows on the listener, and the 7/4 time becomes as plain and pleasant as day. The verse chord progressions are mostly [G], [D], [G], [D], but when unexpected [C] and [Am] chords are thrown in the mix, it almost feels like the song has shifted gears, somehow. That simple change-up is arguably the most memorable part of the album, besides its surprisingly effective lyrics. The transition between the second chorus and the bridge does feel a tad strange somehow,even after multiple listens, but it serves well enough. Interesting flourishes in this song include some staccato strings on the bridge and an interesting flute section during the second verse. Arguably, The most memorable part of the song takes place on the second verse where the [C] and [Am] chords are thrown in amidst the [G] and [D] chords and Matt Berninger begins \\"Huddle with them all night long ...\\" It's so simple, but it really, really works.<br /><br />VERDICT: It's definitely not ear candy to begin with, but once the tricky 7/4 begins to feel natural and the lyrics become noticed and memorized, it's a beautiful, high-caliber song and an unexpected single (and unexpected earworm). It's an absolutely essential listen, and all things considered, it may be The National's most daring song ever.<br /><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"I can't fight it anymore / I'm going through an awkward phase\\"<br />2.) \\"All my drowning friends can see / Now there is no running from it\\"<br />3.) \\"Huddle with them all night long / The worried talk to God goes on\\"<br />4.) \\"I sincerely tried to love it / Wish that I could rise above it\\"<br />5.) \\"When I walk into a room / I do not light it up / F***\\"<br /><br />----SONG 3: \\"Don't Swallow the Cap\\"<br /><br />Written in [F Major], the third song is the second single and is more immediately enjoyable than the previous song on the album. The song is a good listen every time, although it doesn't feel as wholesome as the other songs, in a way. With interesting flourishes and very clever lyrics, this song provides a fast, fun contrast to the previous and following songs (and with fantastic drumming by Bryan Devendorf).<br /><br />ANALYSIS:  There's nothing terribly fancy about the structure here: basic 4/4 time with pleasant chord progressions. Really, the song's strength lies in its flourishes and lyrical prowess. Quick, rapid-fire staccato strings grace the song throughout, giving the song a rather uplifting feel. Additionally, Sufjan Stevens' synths work well on this album, especially near the beginning. The lyrics here are some of the strongest on the album, which is saying quite a lot, taking into account the lyrical strength throughout this album (and The National's previous albums). The title itself might be a reference to Tennessee Williams who choked to death on the cap of a bottle, but the lyrics seem tell a different story, whatever it may be. Somehow, the most memorable part of the song seems to be at the beginning of the outro right after Matt Berninger sings \\"... Or Nevermind\\" at around 3:36. There's something about that part, possibly Bryan Devendorf's intensified drumming, that really sticks.<br /><br />VERDICT: Fast, immediately enjoyable, and maybe more fitting of a single than the previous song on the album, this song adds to the album nicely with sharp lyrics and fantastic flourishes throughout. Not the album's greatest song, but the album definitely wouldn't feel the same without it.<br /><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"Tiny bubbles hang above me / It's a sign that someone loves me\\"<br />2.) \\"I have only two emotions / Careful fear and dead devotion\\"<br />3.) \\"I see all the ones I wept for / All the things I had it in for\\"<br />4.) \\"When they ask what do I see / I say a bright white beautiful heaven hangin' over me\\"<br />5.) \\"I need somewhere to be / But I can't get around the river in front of me\\"<br /><br />----SONG 4: \\"Fireproof\\"<br /><br />Written in [G# Minor/Ab Minor], the fourth song and shortest song on the album isn't immediately recognizable as one of the best. When the unbelievably chilling chorus comes, however, it's immediately apparent. With absolutely spot-on, haunting flourishes, some tricky rhythms in the beginning, and fantastic arrangements all around, there's nothing like the two 2 minutes and 58 seconds this song displays.<br /><br />ANALYSIS:  In the song's intro, there's a tricky melody being played on the guitar. It's initially a little confusing tocount out, but once Matt Berninger begins to sing, things begin to align. While the instrumentation at this point is pleasant, a guitar and piano woven together, the real magic happens once the chorus hits: Right after Matt Berninger sings \\"You're fireproof,\\" Sufjan Stevens adds a low, brooding synth that completely changes a song that begins as mellow as water into something that sounds like it's about to catch fire. Lyrically, the song isn't astounding, and decidedly has the least notable lyrics on the album. However, the song is riddled with beautiful, impeccable flourishes that overshadow the lyrics by miles (the string and flute sections that keep the song from circling its prey in the abyss, for example). The most memorable part of the song is, no doubt, Sufjan Stevens' simple addition to the chorus. It's unforgettable.<br /><br />VERDICT: Short but barely sweet, this song is haunting, spellbinding, mellow, and beautiful all at once. Listening to this song feels somewhat like being hunted by sharks in an ocean (at night) or stalked by tigers in a jungle (at night). It's hard to describe, but it's a must listen.<br /><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"You're fireproof / Nothing breaks your heart\\"<br />2.) \\"You're a needle in the hay / You're the water at the door / You're a million miles away / Doesn't matter anymore\\"<br /><br />----SONG 5: \\"Sea of Love\\"<br /><br />Written in [C Major](?), the fifth song on the album is very surprising structure-wise, wonderfully bombastic, emotionally charged, and lyrically touching (and clever). The emotional highlight of the album, the song features beautiful flourishes and an overall sound that grows on the listener.<br /><br />ANALYSIS: While the song is in 4/4, it presents a structure that really makes it stand out among the other songs: the songs contains only 5 bar chord progressions and 3 bar chord progressions (rather than the usual 4 bars that most songs have). Despite the complexity, the progressions flow seamlesslyand are nearly unnoticeable. The song goes through numerous phases, increasing and dropping in intensity, ultimately building to an absolutely astonishing finish that is beautiful, powerful, and awe-inspiring. The strong, clever lyrics supplement the song wonderfully, and seem to indicate a sort of uncertain father-son relationship (possibly akin to Harry Chapin's \\"Cat's in the Cradle\\", but this is merely speculation), or possibly a bond between to friends. In this extremely diverse song, the most memorable part of the song is when the ascent to the massive finisher begins with background vocals accompanying Matt Berninger (singing \\"Hey Joe ...\\") at around 2:00. In what feels like a sad, strained song, it provides an odd feeling of consolation, like a warm embrace of sorts. Sufjan Stevens' very sublte synth flourishes also make this part extremely memorable and add a very uplifting feeling, though it may take several listens to notice them. It all comes together wonderfully.<br /><br />VERDICT: A grower, this song carries a bittersweet feel amidst the pounding of drums, the tricky chord progressions, the clever lyrics, and the massive ascent to what feels like a kind of Pyrrhic victory. It's not quite a tear-jerker of a song, but it's ultimately the most emotive, awe-inspiring, and explosive song on the album. Highly recommended.<br /><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"If I stay here trouble will find me / If I stay here I'll never leave\\"<br />2.) \\"Joe I'll always think of you / As the kind of child who knew / This was never gonna last / Joe you fell so fast\\"<br />3.) \\"Hey Joe sorry I hurt you but / They say love is a virtue / Don't they?\\"<br />4.) \\"I see you rushing down / Tell me how to reach you / I see you rushing now / What'd Harvard teach you?\\"<br />5.) \\"Can't stay here anymore / We're turning into fiends\\"<br /><br />----SONG 6: \\"Heavenfaced\\"<br /><br />Written in [C Major], the sixth song on the album is slow, fairly somber, and sounds a tad tricky, despite its relatively simple composition and decidedly sad yet hopeful lyrics. It also contains some string flourishes that give the song a unique ending that contrasts (yet mirrors) the power of the previous song.<br /><br />ANALYSIS: The song is in a breezy 3/4 time, but still, it feels oddly tricky at times. It's kind of hard to describe, but after multiple listens, it becomes natural enough. Lyrically, the song has some pretty clever moments and interesting passages worth noting. There's a lonesome feeling to this song that feels oddly akin to... martyrdom. Possibly the end of the world where everyone is waiting to die. It's odd, but the closing lyrics really drive the feeling in. The most memorable part of the song, arguably, is near the end, where inspiring and unexpected string sections help build the song up to a hopeful, though not explosive, ending, right after Berninger sings \\"Because we'll all arrive in heaven alive / we'll all arrive.\\" It feels sort of like a subtle, understated ascent into heaven itself, like martyrdom or death was achieved. It feels cathartic.<br /><br />VERDICT: A pleasant song with some clever string flourishes at the end and some nice lyrics, it's an easy-going listen that sounds like a kind of cleansing from troubles.<br /><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"I could walk out, but I won't / In my mind I am in your arms\\"<br />2.) \\"Let's go wait out in the fields with the ones we love\\"<br />3.) \\"Because we'll all arrive in heaven alive / We'll all arrive\\"<br /><br />----SONG 7: \\"This is the Last Time\\"<br /><br />Written in [C Dorian](?), song seven on the album has a unique, bitter feel to it. The lyrics don't jump out as much as the lyrics on several of the other songs, but the rolling feel of the drums mixed with instantly memorable melodies and arrangements make this song work nicely.<br /><br />ANALYSIS: Immediately catchy and a good listen every go-round (the drumming really powers this song through its collected yet stormy feel), the songworks well with its chord progressions, its somber, orchestral qualities, and instantly enjoyable melodies. The lyrics aren't magnificent, but a few select lyrics do catch attention. They seem to indicate a story of failed love between two lovers: the man who wants his woman back despite the troubles, and the woman who doesn't reciprocate his feelings (The man seems to eventually give up in the end). The feeling of the song is very bitter and romantically miserable. The most memorable part of the song is when Annie Clark (aka St. Vincent) provides background vocals in the outro (\\"It takes a lot of rain in the cup ...\\"). The whole outro is supplemented with orchestral arrangements that seem to drown in wine, tears, and regrets, but when Annie joins in for the frictional duet, it feels like the female personality has revealed herself... and still doesn't want the male lover back. It's a sad situation amidst the beautiful arrangements.<br /><br />VERDICT: The drumming carries this song through nicely, accompanied by its subtle chamber arrangements and fair lyrics that paint a picture of a miserable duo who just couldn't make their ends meet in the end. A good addition to the album.<br /><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"Oh, but your love is such a swamp / You don't think before you jump / And I said I wouldn't get sucked in\\"<br />2.) \\"Oh, don't tell anyone I'm here / I got Tylenol and beer\\"<br />3.) \\"We were so under the brine / We were so vacant and kind\\"<br />4.) \\"Baby you gave me bad ideas / Baby you left me sad and high\\"<br /><br />----SONG 8: \\"Graceless\\"<br /><br />Written in [E Major], song eight and a fan favorite possesses strong lyrics, unrelenting force, fantastic composition and flourishes, and a fast tempo. A wildly fun listen every time, this song builds to a bombastic finale and again displays Matt Berninger's widened vocal range, just like on the first song. This song is an immediate highlight.<br /><br />ANALYSIS: Granted, the National have done a lotof experimentation on this album, especially with time signatures, but this song is in a basic 4/4 with simple-but-fitting chord progressions. The way it's delivered is exceptional. The song is fast, pummeling, uplifting, and laden with fantastic and funny lyrics that seem to tell a story of a miserable or addicted man (or a man who became miserable after losing his woman and turned to medication, maybe). Beneath the party of speed and uplifting chords and melodies is Bryan Devendorf's drumming at its most vicious. The most memorable part of the song starts at around 2:11 where Berninger begins to sign \\"All my thoughts of you ...\\" (Bryan Devendorfs repeated snare hits lead up to the part which really add). The loudest or most grandiose moments of a song aren't necessarily the ones that stick the most, and that simple change-up, which in itself is barely a change-up, gives an oddly dynamic effect to the song that adds a lot to the sound.<br /><br />VERDICT: Loud, fast, ridiculously fun, heavy with wicked drumming, and with miserable and funny lyrics, this song is an explosive addition to the album. This one is worth putting on repeat.<br /><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"Graceless / Is there a powder to erase this? / Is it dissolvable and tasteless? / You can't imagine how I hate this / Graceless\\"<br />2.) \\"There's a science to walking through windows\\"<br />3.) \\"All of my thoughts of you / Bullets through rotten fruit / Come apart at the seams / Now I know what dying means\\"<br />4.) \\"I am not my rosy self / Left my roses on my shelf / Take the white ones, they're my favorites / It's the side effects that save us\\"<br />5.) Grace / Put the flowers you find in a vase / If you're dead in the mind it'll brighten the place / Don't let them die on the vine, it's a waste / Grace<br /><br />----SONG 9: \\"Slipped\\"<br /><br />Written in [E Major], the ninth song has an easygoing, very melancholy sound, decidedly reminiscent of one of the National's earlier songs, \\"Runaway.\\" While lyrics aren't too bad and the instrumentation pretty nice, the song hides a secret: it is one of, if not the, most complex songs on the album. Pay attention to those time signatures.<br /><br />ANALYSIS: Slow and steady, its swing feel compliments its instrumentation. The song is paired with some nice lyrics, although the album contains stronger ones (Whatever story is being told through them doesn't seem to point to anything terribly positive). The simplicity of it all masks the underlying complexity of the song itself, where time signatures blend together so seamlessly, it's downright brilliant. The song is mostly in 7/4, but for a few bars it switches to 4/4, THEN to a few bars of 6/4... and then brains are fried.  There may be other time signatures as well, but wherever they are, if they are, they're unbelievably well disguised. Amidst the mastery of time signature smoke and mirrors, the most memorable part of the song is around 3:25 where things begin to swell as Matt Berninger begins \\"I keep coming back here ...\\" for the final time. Admittedly, the song doesn't contain many memorable moments, but here it feels like the ascent of the hill is over and all that's left to do is overlook the stretch of land and feel the wind blow.<br /><br />VERDICT: This song, on one hand, isn't much to appreciate to begin with. On the other hand, it may be the song worth most of the listener's appreciation.  Despite fair lyrics and simple instrumentation, everything works on a level that is greater than it seems. This song deserves attention.<br /><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"I'm in the city you hated / My eyes are falling / Counting the clicks with the living dead / My eyes are red\\"<br />2.) \\"I'm having trouble inside my skin / I'll try to keep my skeletons in\\"<br />3.) \\"But I'll never be / Anything you ever want me to be\\"<br />4.) \\"I keep coming back here where everything... slipped\\"<br /><br />----SONG 10: \\"I Need My Girl\\"<br /><br />Written in [D#/Eb Major], the tenth song has very somber, somewhat haunting, single-worthy feel to it. The composition is in a slow and steady 4/4 time with sparse yet memorable instrumentation and some pretty flourishes throughout. The lyrics aren't terribly strong. However, they do feel somewhat akin to a comedy-drama.<br /><br />ANALYSIS: The song's structure and composition really make it feel like it could serve as a single. In a slow, simple 4/4, the cold, midnight rain shower song is filled with instantly memorable melodies and flourishes that bring about a very solemn feeling, despite the major key the song is written in. Amidst the pretty guitars, Sufjan Stevens' synths, and all the orchestrations is a collection of lyrics that, while not fantastic, are humorous, strange, and sad. They seem to tell a story of a man who can't be anywhere without his girl... and he's somewhere without his girl. Possibly. In a song that seems to drown in misery, the most memorable part of the song is the outro, where Sufjan Stevens' fantastic synths feel like one final, conclusive gust of wind from a cold front. It's absolutely chilling and it lingers long after the song is over. The littlest of flourishes can make a song, and this is one of them.<br /><br />VERDICT: Perhaps not the most exciting song on the album, it works well with its position on the album and is still a good listen. The ending can send chills down the listener's spine, and is the definitive moment of the song. For lovers of sad or bittersweet music, this one's a winner.<br /><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"I am good, I am grounded / Davy says that I look taller / But I can't get my head around it / I keep feeling smaller and smaller\\"<br />2.) \\"Remember when you lost your s*** and / Drove the car into the garden\\"<br />3.) \\"There's some things that I should never / Laugh about in front of family\\"<br /><br />----SONG 11: \\"Humiliation\\"<br /><br />Written in [A#/Bb Major](?), the eleventh and longest song transitions seamlessly from the somber tenth song and provides uplifting acceleration. The lyrics are decidedly the most humorous on the album, and along with some great chord and key changes (and a fantastic, bluesy ending), this song is a melancholy (yet somewhat carefree) tour-de-force that really surprises... and really sticks.<br /><br />ANALYSIS: Like the third and eighth songs, this song is in 4/4. And fast. Bryan Devendorf's drumming isn't complex here, but it drives the song along, again, like songs three and eight. Above the rolling drums, piano chords and a synth pad provided by Sufjan Steven intertwine to fill the song with a very uplifting and fresh air (like a non-headache inducing mixture of perfume and cologne). It's worth noting that this song introduces brief but very interesting key changes beginning at around 1:58. The song does have some funny lyrics, and whatever story is being conveyed through them is delivered √† la dark humor, and it works well. The most memorable part of the song is also the most surprising part of the song: the bluesy ending, beginning with the notes D#, E, F, G being played on the guitar, and Matt Berninger seemingly singing about a woman who decided that she just wants to have a good time (\\"She wore blue velvet ...\\"). That part sticks from the first listen on. It's completely unexpected, but it completely works. It's the part that the listener waits for every time.<br /><br />VERDICT: A definite go-to song, it's fast, simple, beautiful, funny, and wonderfully arranged with some interesting chord progressions and key changes throughout. However, the ending completely makes the song what it is: fun and addictive. This song is definitely worth a listen. Or two. Or twenty.<br /><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"I survived the dinner / And the air went thinner\\"<br />2.) \\"If I die this instant / Taken from a distance / They will probably list it down / Among other things round town\\"<br />3.) \\"In the skies over black Venice / I see eyes of a white menace\\"<br />4.)\\"All the L.A. women / Fall asleep while swimming / I got paid to fish 'em out / And then one day I lost the job<br />5.) \\"Tunnel vision lights my way / Leave a little life today\\"<br />6.) \\"Under the withering white skies of humiliation\\"<br />7.) \\"She wore blue velvet / Said she can't help it\\"<br /><br />----SONG 12: \\"Pink Rabbits\\"<br /><br />Written in [A Major], the penultimate song on the album displays the best lyrics on the entire album, no questions asked. Its lyrics paired with its simple composition give the song a curiously magical feeling that's hard to describe. This song is also the album's weed: it grows much faster than expected.<br /><br />ANALYSIS: Compositionally, the song is simple and pretty enough in a pinch. It's in an easy, swung 4/4 time with a very unassuming chord progression, but it wouldn't be able to stand on its own. And it doesn't: the lyrics on this song are really the best of the thirteen songs. Once heard and remembered, the lyrics completely change the feeling of the song and instill a feeling of magic mixed with sadness. The lyrics seem to tell a bittersweet story of two off-kilter lovers (or ex-lovers who long to return to each other, maybe). Whatever the song is about, it feels akin to opposing magnetic fields in love, and it sounds absolutely wonderful. The most memorable part of the song is, again, the outro. At around 3:30 is when Matt reiterates the final lyrics of the song (\\"You said it would be painless ...\\"), accompanied by celeste notes that are really the icing on the cake. The sound is unified, nostalgic, awe-inspiring, incredible.<br /><br />VERDICT: A big surprise on the album, this song's stunning and adventurous lyrics coaxes the simple arrangements its married to out of its humble state to fly together over incredible distances and above mountainous peaks. It only gets better after each listen, and it definitely deserves the listener's attention. It also deserves to be called one of the best songs on the album.<br/><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"It wasn't like a rain, it was more like a sea / I didn't ask for this pain, it just came over me\\"<br />2.) \\"I'm so surprised you want to dance with me now / I was just getting used to living life without you around / I'm so surprised you want to dance with me now / You always said I held you way too high off the ground\\"<br />3.) \\"You didn't see me I was falling apart / I was a white girl in a crowd of white girls in the park / You didn't see me I was falling apart / I was a television version of a person with a broken heart\\"<br />4.) \\"You said it would be painless / A needle in a doll / You said it would be painless / It wasn't that at all\\"<br />5.) \\"Somebody said you disappeared in a crowd / I didn't understand then / I don't understand now<br /><br />----SONG 13: \\"Hard to Find\\"<br /><br />Written in [G# Major], the closing song on the album ends the collection very solemnly and beautifully. This song has fairly nice lyrics, and like most National songs, it's a bit of a grower. This song is also the final testament that, yes, the National have done more experimenting with music than usual this time around.<br /><br />ANALYSIS: One thing isn't immediately apparent about the song: it isn't in 4/4 time. Rather, it's seamlessly composed in 5/4 time, aided by the easygoing feel throughout the song. The composition is so smooth that there's little or nothing to \\"get used to,\\" in a sense, with this time signature. Though the lyrics themselves aren't terribly stunning, there are some clever moments throughout, and even a reference to the Violent Femmes. The most memorable part of the song is, like many of the others, the outro. Beginning around 3:09, the band introduces a more present drumming pattern and a beautiful, regal brass section that sounds like a memorial to a dead, beloved king. It's subtle, yet somewhat breathtaking and bittersweet.<br /><br />VERDICT: Not immediately loveable, but after a few listens, the song reveals the qualities of its composition and overall sound. A very beautiful, mellow way to end a stunning album.<br /><br />STAND-OUT LYRICS:<br />1.) \\"They can all / Just kiss off into the air\\"<br />2.) \\"Don't know why we had to lose / The ones who took so little space / We're still waiting for the ease / To cover what we can't erase\\"<br />3.) \\"I'm not holding out for you / But I'm still watching for the signs / If I tried you'd probably be / Hard to find\\"<br /><br />In the end, no song is really a let down. Though some are definitively better than others, Each song contains things worth appreciating, whether they be tiny flourishes, fast tempos, slow tempos, fantastic lyrics, mind-frying time signatures, key changes, ideal chord progressions, or the like. The National may have traded their stress levels for wild, hidden-in-plain-sight ideas and there doesn't seem to be a real misstep. Even though some songs work really well enough alone, everything works together wonderfully in the long run. Here's to another fantastic album by The National.<br /><br />Personal favorite song: \\"Humiliation\\"

0316187216|4.0|\\"Where I come from we got a different way of doing things.\\"<br /><br />- Lamb<br /><br />I think what separates Joe Abercrombie's characters from so many others we see in fantasy fiction (and real life, for that matter) is the incredible self awareness each character possesses, from the primary protagonists all the way down to the lowliest henchman. Abercrombie's greatest strength is creating these world-weary characters who just don't have the energy to sugarcoat things any more, and this unflinching honesty not only extends to the world and the people that surround them, but to their own faults and inadequacies.<br /><br />In RED COUNTRY, Abercrombie actually introduces two characters that, with the possible exception of the Dogman from previous novels, seem the least likely to engage in sociopathic tendencies. Shy South, a woman whose younger brother and sister have been kidnapped, has a past that includes theft and murder, but those days seem to be in her past. As we get to know her during the events covered in the novel, she's capable, but she's often shocked by the violence of her compatriots and the wasteful deaths that plague her rescue efforts.<br /><br />Meanwhile, Temple, a self-professed coward who admits to taking the easy way out whenever it's available, actually isn't that bad a person. He's cowardly, but his heart's generally in the right place. That makes him a tremendous deviation from many of the other characters Abercrombie has introduced us to over the years, especially the apparent good guys that often prove just as bad as their adversaries.<br /><br />Unfortunately, I didn't find Shy and Temple to be as interesting as some of the other, less sane characters from previous Abercrombie novels. Fortunately, many of my favorite sociopaths return, including Caul Shivers, Friendly (possibly my favorite character in the Abercrombie-verse), Nicomo Cosca and Lamb, a nine-fingered Northman who's new name certainly won't fool readers who have read Abercrombie's previous works -- though interestingly enough, the character's real name is never actually mentioned.<br /><br />The fantasy western conceit works well with Abercrombie's strengths of playing with common tropes and utilizing the aforementioned world-weary characters. (Perhaps I like my characters slightly insane.) The plot moves a bit slowly and I didn't find these characters as gripping as I have in Abercrombie's previous books, but the POV characters' voices still provide the story with a sturdy backbone that makes it better than most everything else out there.

B0064NTZJO|5.0|Believe it. Drive is the best film to come along in a very long time. Not since Pulp Fiction has there been a more passionate love letter to the Cinema. Drive is one of my favorite movies of the millennium era and it gives me hope... Cinema may not be completely dead after all.<br /><br />Ryan Gosling stars as the Driver in yet another performance that has helped to cement him as one of Hollywood's top actors. Nicolas Winding Refn directs him with ease. With Drive, Refn has proven himself as one the premier directors of his generation. Drive, along with his previous films like Bronson and the Pusher trilogy, is not necessarily a reinvention of modern cinema, but more or less a reinterpretation of the days of Cinema past. Refn is startlingly good, so good I get goosebumps just from watching his scenes take time and build up. Finally the likes of Scorsese and Cronenberg can take it easy, and Tarantino and the Coens can step aside to make way for the next great talent in film direction: Nicolas Winding Refn.<br /><br />Drive takes its lead from, quite possibly, the coolest film ever made. Jean-Pierre Melville's 1967 magnum opus Le Samourai.<br /><br />Refn's creation, the character of the Driver, is cut straight from the same cloth as Le Samourai's Jef Costello. The characters share several likenesses, the least important of which being a mysterious and sordid past. The most important likeness being the almost ritualistic way both men carry out the tasks assigned to them, the lonely lives in which they traverse through a disconnected and oblivious habitat, the trust that doesn't come easy but when it does it is given to a pretty girl who seals both of their fates, and most interestingly, the eyes. Ryan Gosling must have studied Alain Delon's performance as they both seem to communicate most of the plot-points with their eyes and only their eyes. A subtle physicality that is unbelievably difficult to perpetuate freely comes naturally to the tortured stare found in bothDelon and Gosling. The Driver's real name could easily have been Jef Costello as far as I'm concerned. This is also telling of how many different ways the same film can be so influential to extremely different ends. This polarity is best explored by Drive and John Woo's 1989 The Killer. Both films are flamboyantly open about the debt each owes to the influence of Le Samourai. Where Drive is almost an arthouse action film, the Killer is the exact opposite of that. But its influence on both films is unmistakable, Le Samourai wrote the book, and Refn has memorized each and every passage. Yes, it doesn't just stop with Gosling's performance, Drive owes a great deal to Le Samourai in almost every single department. The pacing Refn embraces is classic Melville, the way he tells the story is almost formulaic of a Melville crime picture. Drive, in essence, is an American action film with 60s French sensibilities. People may seem to give Drive too much credit in the originality department, where much of the credit is Melville-deserved. But I don't hold anything against Drive for being almost a reinterpretation Le Samourai. I see Drive for what it was always meant to be, the warmest and most sincere thank you letter to classic Cinema ever filmed. Refn does no hiding when it comes to the Melville influence, its there staring you proudly in the face. Bravo, Drive, Bravo! Melville would be proud.<br /><br />What is most unique about Drive is the fact that the film is just as much a salute to the French New Wave of the sixties as it is a reimagining of the mainstream American cinema of the eighties. Surprisingly enough, in a film that boasts a very large and formidable Arthouse following, 1986's Cobra, 1985's To Live And Die In L.A., and several other nods to 80s cinema is almost interchangeable with the 60s influence. Where the 60s influence may be more subtle and, inherently, much more consistent throughout, the 80s influence is mostly on the surface. The 80s influence in Driveis of the strictly superficial variety, which is both faithful and respectful of the 80s films and techniques certain areas of the film are modeled after. Neon pink title cards, synthesizer soundtrack, mood changes, the poster art, and the occasional trademark 80s lighting. Although a caricature like Marion 'Cobra' Cobretti can in fact be found on the surface of the character of the Driver, that is only the surface, it is not the actual substance of the character. The substance of the character is Le Samourai's Jef Costello. Its almost as if the movie's true colors are of the Melville variety, but it masquerades itself as something much more accessible. Drive pretends to be an 80s American action film. But Drive is so much than that, as any semi-educated cinefile will tell you, Drive is film that wears several different masks. Its true identity is that of Melville. And I can't think of a better frame of mind than that.<br /><br />Cast-wise, yes, Albert Brooks steals the show. The character Brooks plays is also one of the only non-french influences on the film. Bernie Ross is wholly American, and that is why he is such a vicious character. Bernie Ross is an 80s action villain and he is pitted against a 60s character study. The clash is almost inevitable but the mutual respect contained therein is the most rewarding aspect of their relationship. Much the same can be said about fans of the Arthouse and of the Mainstream. There is a mutual respect there, although all too often these sects of film fans and filmmakers will clash, they are nothing without the other. That said, in theory, the rift between Bernie Ross and the Driver can be symbolic of several different things: Mainstream vs Arthouse, Excess vs Conservation, Action vs Meditation, European sensibilities vs American know-how, but most daringly it represents the Death Of Modern Cinema. All the greats are killing each other off. Film cannot survive in a climate of servitude to the past. Mainstream wounds the Arthouse, the Arthouse fights back. Both the Mainstream and the Arthouse refuse to let go of their history, therefore they are doomed to tell the same stories over and over again, and cut the other's throat when necessary. The current state of filmmaking is in a very sad state of affairs as of late, Drive is a film that pits over-used caricatures, genres, and eras, popular of modern visual storytelling, against each other, until once everything is done, destroyed, and wounded. All you are left with is the end credits and the promise of a stripped down and re-imagined future of filmmaking.

B009AMANH4|1.0|This will be a very brief review. Steven Spielberg was obviously Oscar-hungry during the production of Lincoln. I'm not sure if Spielberg ever had a brave bone in his body, but after watching Lincoln, I wonder if he even has a soul. Lincoln is a soulless film. It is very difficult to make a film that deals with all of the interesting historical events that Lincoln is centered around boring, but Spielberg seems to do that effortlessly. What's more, Daniel Day Lewis churns out a boring and half-hearted performance, as do most of the actors in the piece! Its as if Lincoln was made by a half-interested company for a built in audience that were completely uninterested in fanciful concepts like intimacy, empathy, and courage.<br /><br />Lincoln is not a film, it is an instructional video for all future hack-job directors on how to make a movie about something as multi-faceted as the presidency of Abraham Lincoln seem watered-down, hokey, and as accessible to as many people as possible. Ken Burns should have been an adviser on the film in some capacity, as he seems to know a thing or two about intimacy and courage. 1907523685|5.0|No lover in the world ever wrote a valentine more exquisite than Virginia Woolf's tribute to her lover Vita Sackville-West. That tribute was \\"Orlando: A Biography,\\" a magical-realism tale about a perpetually youthful, charming hero/ine who traverses three centuries and both genders -- and Woolf's writing reaches a new peak as she explores the hauntingly sensuous world of Orlando.<br /><br />Orlando was born a young aristocratic man in the time of Queen Elizabeth I, and when the dying monarch visited his home she became his new court favorite (and briefly lover). His passionate, curious personality attracted many other women over the years -- until he fell in love with Sasha, a mercurial but faithless Russian princess (supposedly based on Sackville-West's ex-girlfriend). Bereft of true love, he devoted himself to poetry and entertainment.<br /><br />But then he's assigned to be an ambassador to Constantinople, and something strange happens -- while a bloody revolution rages, he sleeps for a full week... and wakes newly metamorphosed into a woman. With the same mind and soul but a female body, Orlando sets out on a new life -- and discovers that women aren't quite as different from men as she once thought.<br /><br />\\"Orlando: A Biography\\" is a very weird book, and was even more so when it was written since \\"magical realism\\" didn't exist as a literary style in 1928. Virginia Woolf makes no explanations about Orlando's immortality or unexpected gender switch. It's simply accepted that once he was a man, and then she became a woman, and that s/he has lived from the Elizabethan era until at least the 1920s (and who knows, maybe Orlando still wanders among us?).<br /><br />And Woolf's writing is at its peak here. Her prose is soaked in luxurious descriptions that constantly tease the senses -- silver and gold, frozen flowers, crystalline ice, starlight and the exquisite expanses of nature's beauty. At times the sensual writing seems almost feverish, and Woolf adds an almost mythic quality by inserting spirits of feminine virtues (Modesty, Purity and Chastity appear to try to hide Orlando's feminine body), and by having her hero/ine encounter great poets, queens and men of the sea.<br /><br />And Orlando him/herself is a truly fascinating character -- s/he can be sweet, passionate, romantic, wild and melancholy, and s/he has an almost magnetic charisma. He starts off the story as an elusive romantic teenager, suffers a heartbreak that matures him as an artist, and post-metamorphosis she becomes a woman of the modern world. Both in mind and body, Orlando is a very different woman at the end than the boy s/he began as.<br /><br />\\"Orlando: A Biography\\" is a truly spellbinding book -- Virginia Woolf's prose enthralls the senses while her main character explores the boundaries of gender. A must-read, for everyone.st mythic quality by inserting spirits of feminine virtues (Modesty, Purity and Chastity appear to try to hide Orlando's feminine body), and by having her hero/ine encounter great poets, queens and men of the sea.<br /><br />And Orlando him/herself is a truly fascinating character -- s/he can be sweet, passionate, romantic, wild and melancholy, and s/he has an almost magnetic charisma. 1594746168|4.0|They say not to judge a book by its cover. But with the cover of \\"The Resurrectionist: The Lost Work of Dr. Spencer Black,\\" what you see is what you get.<br /><br />And to be honest, the picture of a winged-humanoid skeleton, with every bone carefully catalogued, was enough to reel me into checking out this book. It's a pseudo-biography of a fictional man who devoted himself to the scientific study of ancient mythical creatures -- and while E.B. Hudspeth spins a fine fictional biography, the illustrations are what really took my breath away.<br /><br />The book tells the story of Spencer Black, a 19th-century physician whose father was a grave-robbing professor of anatomy. That fascination with anatomy carried over into Black's career -- first he became fascinated by transformation in the insect world, and then by the workings of the human body. But when he encounters the corpse of a \\"fawn-child,\\" his research took an unexpected turn.<br /><br />After that, Dr. Black came up with a shocking, controversial theory: that mythical creatures were not only real, but were ancestors of humanity. According to him, birth defects were just those ancient genetic traits trying to resurface. So he tried to create his own \\"mythical\\" creatures by grafting together body parts from different animals -- which, unsurprisingly, the scientific community was unimpressed by.<br /><br />The late 19th century is a perfect era for the fictional Dr. Black -- it was a time of massive technological advances and strange new pseudosciences. Just think of the Fiji Mermaid. So while \\"The Resurrectionist: The Lost Work of Dr. Spencer Black\\" is an entirely fictional work, EB Hudspeth manages to blur the boundaries between fiction and reality -- you can almost believe it is a biography of a real person.<br /><br />He also does an excellent job writing a pseudo-biography, exploring the events in Black's life (failed surgery, death of his children) that fueled his obsessions. Hudspeth even writes letters to/from Black, as well as a journal entry from his brother Bernard about his first, horrifying \\"graft.\\"<br /><br />But the most fascinating part of the book is not the fictional biography, but the \\"The Codex Extinct Animalia.\\" In this, we can see beautifully detailed drawings of sphinxes, harpies, fluttering multi-finned mermaids, dragons (serpentine and regular), pegasi, and countless other mythical creatures. Not only are these the most realistic depictions of mythical creatures I have ever seen, but they are the most scientifically plausible.<br /><br />Hudspeth achieves this by examining these creatures down the muscles, organs and bones, which are catalogued in painstaking detail. He even catalogues them by different orders and fictional families -- for instance, the Siren Oceanus is a member of the family Sirenidae and the genus Siren, with internal lungs covered in gills.<br /><br />\\"The Resurrectionist: The Lost Work of Dr. Spencer Black\\" is an exquisite piece of work -- a solid, sometimes horrifying pseudo-biography, followed by exquisitely realistic depictions of mythic creatures. If nothing else, read this for Hudspeth's beautiful illustrations.to/from Black, as well as a journal entry from his brother Bernard about his first, horrifying \\"graft.\\"<br /><br />But the most fascinating part of the book is not the fictional biography, but the \\"The Codex Extinct Animalia.\\" In this, we can see beautifully detailed drawings of sphinxes, harpies, fluttering multi-finned mermaids, dragons (serpentine and regular), pegasi, and countless other mythical creatures. B00168HARG|4.0|I should note that I'm a huge fan of the franchise, even of extensions that others don't like (such as the third film, or the games). As such I'm more open-minded than most, and I've noticed more than a few things about this series that others have been missing.<br /><br />First off the premise, and the descriptions given by others, can be very misleading. This series is by no means a 'true sequel to T2', nor does it replace or ignore T3. This series is really a sequel to the established story. It alters the timeline from a previous point and causes the entire story to scew into something completely new. If you really want to get the most out of this series, watch it after viewing the films (and if you want, playing the games).<br /><br />Secondly, the series itself does have some flaws that could be deal breakers to some. For one thing, you'd best be a fan of modern 'indy' filming. The series will barrage you with a series of split second shots and unsteady camera work. Most of the time you won't really be able to tell what's going on. Some people like the energy this style has, others find it confusing and even annoying.<br /><br />The acting is solid all around, but the writing can be so-so. The series bounces between action sci-fi with a well developed plot and a soap opera for teens. It quickly becomes clear that the reason they set the series in modern day and violated the timeline was so they could focus on John's struggle with puberty. As such there will be times that Sara and Co. will be fleeing and fighting various Cyborg death machines and uncovering conspiracies, and other times we watch as John goes to school, feels awkward, and tries to get friends (mostly to get laid). It does have the spirit of the films, but it also has a strong vein of 90210, Beverly Hills, and Buffy the Vampire Slayer.<br /><br />It's still a great show, especially by modern standards. But it is by no means perfect, nor is it set between T2 and T3.

1400069742|5.0|Those Angry Days covers the two years leading up to the U.S. involvement in the Second World War. The fact that it comes after seven decades simply confirms that several generations must pass before many important details surface either from private or government sources. Historian Olson appears to have culled every available source to bring the story into focus. The late distinguished historian, Arthur M. Schlesinger, Jr., once noted \\"History is indeed an argument without end.\\" Indeed it is, but Those Angry Days is about as close to an \\"end\\" of the \\"battle\\" over our involvement in the Second World War: Should America, just emerging from the Great Depression, aid Great Britain with treasure and/or blood as Nazi Germany stood poised to vanquish the island nation? Angry words--and often blunt threats to life and limb- flew between those determined to keep us out of the European conflict and those equally determined to thrust us in. Those \\"angry days\\" ended with the attack on Pearl Harbor followed shortly by Germany's declaration of war on the U.S.<br />  This most certainly should be assigned reading to students as an introduction to the Second World War. Two powerful groups clashed over what would be in the best interests of the nation. But when war came there was but one goal: Victory.

The principal characters have a presence which makes them jump off the page.<br />Travis Maddox is a walking disaster area who earns money in secret fights in the basement of Eastern University, where promoters and students bet on brutal no-rules bare-knuckle fights. Abby Abernathy is brought along by her best friend America, who is going out with Shepley, Travis's cousin. Travis gets blood on Abby's pretty cardigan and gets interested in seeing her again. She's not keen, because now she knows about the gaudy tattoos under his shirt. His nickname 'Mad Dog' isn't encouraging. Not to mention the string of cheap one-night-stands he's famous for.<br />However since Travis rooms with Shepley, America and Abby discover that he cleans up well, can hold a conversation and is a straight A student majoring in criminal law. He got a scholarship for half his tuition and fight fees pay the rest. Abby is good at math but not biology and Travis offers to coach her, nicknaming her Pigeon. She decides that they can be friends but no more. Naturally she's the object of cafeteria gossip and Travis's daunting presence ensures that other boys don't try to chat Abby up, apart from the braver Parker Hayes who dates her and gives her a diamond bracelet, earning Travis's undying enmity.<br />The amoral Travis makes a bet with Abby that if he is hit once in his next fight, he will be celibate for a month, but if he avoids getting hit she has to live with him for a month. He wins and Abby - later we find that her father is addicted to gambling, a past she is trying to escape - feels has to move in with him and Shepley. Refusing to sleep on the couch where Travis has his encounters with women Abby ends up 'having' to share his bed. She could have bought a sleeping bag and chosen the floor. Two weeks later the two are obsessed each other but Abby, even as she watches moody, jealous Travis improve the behaviour he learnt in a dysfunctional family, is terrified of getting trapped with the same drinking, violent and gambling marriage as her mother endured.<br />BEAUTIFUL DISASTER is a catalogue of irresponsible behaviour from driving drunk to causing actual bodily harm in the cafeteria without anyone complaining to the police or university. To the author's credit, there is at least one voice of reason telling a girl that it is suicidal to bet she can drink fifteen shots of tequila, and the aftermath is anything but pleasant. When Travis and Abby eventually become lovers he has nothing going for him but vigour, pleasing the lady never having been his priority; at least he was savvy enough always to use protection. 1451696191|5.0|Stephen Chbosky's, The Perks of Being a Wallflower, published February 1st, 1999, is a well-written, excellent novel that cleverly tells the exhilarating journey of growing up. The novel is written in letter format enabling the reader to observe how Charlie, the protagonist, develops as a character through his letters to an unknown reader. The components of this novel are both interesting and moving; it conveys critical messages that the reader can interpret and relate to. The journey of Charlie's freshman year at high school is fascinating with surprises at every turn. Chbosky writes in the casual yet meaningful style of a teenage boy, dealing with issues from getting a date to dances to abusive relationships; The Perks of Being a Wallflower is one story that won't be put down until the very end.<br />  The Perks of Being a Wallflower has a remarkable plot line, describing the ups and downs of high school, friends, family, and life itself. The story begins with Charlie writing a letter, \\"I just need to know that someone out there listens and understands and doesn't try to sleep with people even if they could have. I need to know that these people exist.\\"(2) It isn't important that the person he is writing to is never revealed; it makes the actual story much more important. The reader sees how a socially awkward freshman makes his first friends, and loses some, while battling problems at home. He meets his best friends Sam and Patrick, and Bill his inspirational English teacher: three people who support, love, and truly understand him. These people help Charlie grow up. He learns to drive, improve in school, and finally kiss Sam. At the end of the novel there is a plot twist explaining in part why he struggles so much to act \\"normal\\". The complex content of this story, including drugs, romance, problems at home, and some pure joy, creates the captivating novel which keeps the reader's eyes glued to the page.<br />  A significant reason for this novel's success is the way the reader can relate to the book. From the Rocky Horror Picture Show and song lyrics to drugs, sex, and the idea that \\"We accept the love we think we deserve\\" (24) this story speaks to a multitude of people. Throughout the story Charlie reads books including To Kill a Mockingbird; which not only relate to aspects in his life, but also draw the reader's attention to recurring themes of human relationships. Chbosky craftily incorporates everyday occurrences while conveying important messages. It is these reading between the line moments, that truly create the perfect story.

B004VQFQ2Q|5.0|Born again was the one album that featured Ian Gillen (Deep Purple) on vocals. While this album is often overshadowed by the Ozzy and Dio era albums in Sabbath's library, it contains some cult classics like, &#34;Zero The Hero&#34; and &#34;Digital Bitch&#34;. Ian Gillen's trademark scream fits in surprisingly well with the doomy atmosphere of the music. This remastered/expanded edition does sound better than the original version, which suffered from a strange murky production. The real treat for fans is the bonus disc which contains one unreleased track from the original album session and a good selection of live cuts featuring Ian on vocals. There is a nice selection of both older Sabbath classics and songs that come from the Born Again era. I personally do not know of any other &#34;official&#34; live recordings of Sabbath from this time period, so it's a welcome addition for true fans. Overall, if you dig Sabbath and you dig Deep Purple, picking up this reissue is a no brainer.

B009ZQ8FQG|5.0|In what can only be described as a sumptuous nod to the BBC series &#34;Upstairs Downstairs&#34; that captivated audiences and stole ratings in the early '70's (1971 to be exact), Dowton Abbey is a vivid, lush, beautifully rendered portrait of a bygone era: of men and women of substance and means, and of whom we will not see the like again. Breathtakingly beautiful, every frame reminds one of the works of portrait artist, John Singer Sargent, indeed, his works may prove to have been the inspiration for the careful art direction. Downton Abby isn't simply a window on the wealth and society of old monied England, and peripherally, America, but it is an honest representation of all that was decent and fine in these Lords and Ladies whoes privalege was never taken for granted, and whoes sense of responsibility bade them care for their fortunes for the fortunes of so many others depended upon their kind and faithful stewardship. In this series, destined to be an instant classic, no stone is left unturned in every minute detail of costume, furnishing, language, wit and scathing criticism. Though lofty, the gentry are human and, if pressed, quite kind. What is fascinating is how disapproving the majority of well-to-do become around, what we would consider to be, the vulgarities of 'new money', or, in this case, newly aquired old money. Downton Abbey is a must see, as it reminds the viewer that, far from stuck-up elitists, these men and women were the substance of the bond that held the Empire together. Insightful, riviting, suspensful and peopled with well-rounded brilliantly crafted characters, Downton Abbey is sure to make it into your DVD library.
